        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202309637')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())

        //     .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-rail-1')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.rail)
            .setTargeting('Position', 'advert-rail-1')
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-rail-2')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.rail)
            .setTargeting('Position', 'advert-rail-2')

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fad27e57f44947c3a0ce2890b24845c7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0bf9b2aa-1d25-4c72-8382-b358fd071b62'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d87477237" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) with high theoretical capacity is viewed as a promising anode for sodium-ion batteries but suffers from inferior rate capability owing to the polaron-induced slow charge transfer. Herein, a polaron collapse strategy induced by electron-rich insertions is proposed to effectively solve the above issue. Specifically, 1D [MoS] chains are inserted into MoS<sub>2</sub> to break the symmetry states of 2D layers and induce small-polaron collapse to gain fast charge transfer so that the as-obtained thermodynamically stable Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> shows metallic behavior with 10<sup>7</sup> times larger electrical conductivity than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>. Theoretical calculations demonstrate that Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> owns highly delocalized anions, which substantially reduce the interactions of Na−S to efficiently accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, endowing Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> lower energy barrier (0.38&nbsp;vs 0.65&nbsp;eV of MoS<sub>2</sub>). The novel Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode exhibits a high capacity of 510 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C and a superior high-rate stability of 217 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 C over 15&nbsp;000 cycles. Further in situ and ex situ characterizations reveal the in-depth reversible redox chemistry in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. The proposed polaron collapse strategy for intrinsically facilitating charge transfer can be conducive to electrode design for fast-charging batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202309637-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202309637-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The carbon neutrality target is pushing the development of economically feasible energy-storage technologies at all scales.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0001_R_d87477204e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are viewed as promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) owing to the natural abundance of sodium resources, which is expected to bring down the manufacturing cost and thus promote device affordability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0002_R_d87477204e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0003_R_d87477204e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the energy-power tradeoff issue commonly seen in LIB systems becomes even more severe for SIB systems, which originates from the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) compared with Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0004_R_d87477204e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0006_R_d87477204e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this sense, rationally designing new electrode materials to fit into the smooth Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation with simultaneously fast-charging and high-capacity behavior is crucial to enabling the commercialization of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0007_R_d87477204e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0009_R_d87477204e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) with a unique layered structure, as an anode candidate for SIBs, has attracted considerable attention due to its high theoretical capacity (670 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0010_R_d87477204e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Sci.">10</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0012_R_d87477204e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the thermodynamically stable 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub> (2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>) shows poor electrical conductivity due to its semiconducting property, triggering slow electron transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0013_R_d87477204e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0014_R_d87477204e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the large volume variation during the cycling process inevitably causes the continuous collapse of the layered structure, resulting in sluggish ion diffusion kinetics and severe capacity fading.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0015_R_d87477204e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0016_R_d87477204e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Considerable efforts based on compositional or structural modification of MoS<sub>2</sub> have been devoted to boosting the electron/ion migration kinetics to achieve favorable sodium storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0017_R_d87477204e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Sci.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conductive substrates (e.g., graphene and MXene) have been commonly incorporated to improve charge transfer and alleviate volume expansion,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0018_R_d87477204e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0022_R_d87477204e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which has been proven to make sense for enhancing rate capability and cycling lifespan. Further, increasing surface defects, enlarging interlayer spacing, as well as constructing heterointerfaces, have been applied to accelerate electrochemical kinetics with good results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0023_R_d87477204e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0025_R_d87477204e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the above strategies typically cost elaborate manufacture with low yield, which inevitably limits their practical applications. Phase transition engineering has been suggested as a valid approach to intrinsically facilitate electron/ion migration for fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0026_R_d87477204e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Energy">26</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0028_R_d87477204e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The adjustable coordination of Mo and S atoms in the crystal structure endows MoS<sub>2</sub> with different electronic characteristics, and unlike semiconducting 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> (1T-MoS<sub>2</sub>) exhibits metallic behavior and a lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0029_R_d87477204e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">29</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0031_R_d87477204e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, because of its metastable nature, 1T-MoS<sub>2</sub> is difficult to synthesize directly, and it tends to convert to the stable 2H phase via restacking during cycling, which causes rapid electrochemical attenuation under high current densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0032_R_d87477204e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0033_R_d87477204e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. - Eur. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it remains a significant challenge to efficiently construct a thermodynamically stable phase with inherent fast electron/ion diffusion kinetics through rational structure design.</p>
         
         <p>From the molecular point of view, Goodenough and co-workers have revealed that polaron and lattice polarization play a significant role in conductivity. In detail, when an excess electron passes through the semiconductor, the self-trapping phenomenon will happen (i.e., the formation of a small polaron; see Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and cause sluggish charge transportation,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0034_R_d87477204e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Phys. Rev. B">34</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0035_R_d87477204e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which accounts for the poor electrical conductivity in 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0036_R_d87477204e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Phys. Chem. C">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, Goodenough and other researchers also suggested that symmetry-breaking states introduced by defects or insertions will induce polaron preemption (or polaron collapse); thus, electron transportation can be accelerated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0037_R_d87477204e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">37</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0038_R_d87477204e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, spin-polarized electrons (i.e., typical symmetry-breaking states) are recently observed in monolayer MoS<sub>2</sub>, which owns a better electrical conductivity than the bulk phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0039_R_d87477204e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, tearing the 2D interlaminar structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> by insertions to induce small-polaron collapse could be an effective strategy to construct a charge transfer-favorable framework. Further, recent studies on lattice dynamics also demonstrated that a delocalized anion (which can be obtained by electron injection) will weaken the bonding interactions between the mobile cations and surrounding anions in crystal material; thus, achieving enhanced ionic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0040_R_d87477204e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0043_R_d87477204e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - eScience">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taken together, inserting electron-rich Mo species to tear the 2D layers of MoS<sub>2</sub> may be a viable approach to obtain a fast-charging SIB anode.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a thermodynamically stable metallic Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was designed by inserting 1D [MoS] chains into the 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> layers via a gram-scale solid-state reaction (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1a</a>). DFT calculations with an excess electron injection revealed that small polaron in MoS<sub>2</sub> (−0.9&nbsp;V vs Fermi level) disappeared after the insertion of [MoS] chains that break the symmetry of 2D layers in the <i>c</i>-direction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1b</a>). As-induced small-polaron collapse in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> liberated electrons from the self-trapped state; and thus, delocalized electron state would be achieved (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1b</a>). This accounts for a 10<sup>7</sup> times higher electrical conductivity in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (4.4 × 10<sup>4</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup>), compared with that in 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (4.5 × 10<sup>−3</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup>). Further theoretical calculations confirmed that Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> owns highly delocalized anions, which substantially reduce the interactions of Na−S to kinetically accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in crystal material with a much lower energy barrier (0.38 vs 0.65&nbsp;eV of MoS<sub>2</sub>). Benefiting from fluent electron/ion transport, reversible redox chemistry in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was revealed by in situ Raman spectra, X-ray diffraction (XRD), and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) characterizations, in which the reconstruction of Mo─S bonds and the structural integrity could be verified. The novel Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode exhibited a high capacity of 510 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C and superior high-rate stability of 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C over 5000 cycles as well as 217 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 C over 15&nbsp;000 cycles, outperforming previously reported advanced SIB anodes (<b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202309637-tbl-0001">1</a>). Further, the potentiality of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> in flexible electronics had been exhibited. This work demonstrates that polaron collapse induced by electron-rich insertions could obtain intrinsic accelerated electron/ion transport, which was expected to supplement an effective electrode design strategy for fast-charging batteries.</p>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202309637-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202309637-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202309637-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202309637-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Predictions of Band Structure and Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Behavior</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to investigate the differences in intrinsic electronic properties, as well as their influences on Na<sup>+</sup> storage behaviors. Based on the modified crystal structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), a band-gap fluctuation could be observed in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, that is, the enlarged band fluctuations of Mo-<i>d</i> orbitals led to a narrowed bandgap (≈0&nbsp;eV, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information) and make Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> a metallic feature. In contrast, the bandgap of MoS<sub>2</sub> could be evaluated to be 1.69&nbsp;eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information). The difference in bandgap suggested a faster carrier transport in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, which may have been introduced by symmetry breaking in the <i>c</i>-direction. With an excess electron injection, the small polaron could be observed in MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information); while, its collapse could also be observed in M<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the projected DOS (PDOS) pattern for S-<i>p</i> orbitals (spin up) of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibited a smaller integral area ratio (i.e., 0.226 ranging from +3 to −3&nbsp;eV) compared to that of MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.364) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). This confirms the existence of a more delocalized anion in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, which may substantially decrease the interactions of Na─S to efficiently accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0048_R_d87477204e1149" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat. Catal.">48</a>-<a href="#adma202309637-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0050_R_d87477204e1149" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To assess the potential for sodium ions storage, a Na atom is then introduced to the slab model of MoS<sub>2</sub> (001) and Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (001) for further calculations. The charge density difference plots obtained from DFT calculations reveal that the Na atom shows less electron transfer (i.e., a lower electron density in the map, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) in Na/Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> compared with that in Na/MoS<sub>2</sub>, indicating the weakened binding strength of Na<sup>+</sup> in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. According to previous studies, the [MoS] insertion (i.e., electron-rich species injection) and as-obtained delocalized anions may account for this phenomenon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0051_R_d87477204e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Sci.">51</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0052_R_d87477204e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Nat. Commun.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the binding energies of Na<sup>+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub> and Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are calculated to be 3.70 and 0.56&nbsp;eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1d</a>). The significantly lower binding energy of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> system demonstrates weaker interactions of Na─S in crystal material, theoretically allowing for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Transition state assessments further illustrate that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier in the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> system is 0.38&nbsp;eV, significantly lower than that in MoS<sub>2</sub> (0.65&nbsp;eV, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Overall, the [MoS] insertion will lead to symmetry breaking and result in a small-polaron collapse that substantially accelerates electron transfer. Meanwhile, the [MoS] insertion can introduce delocalized anions in the crystals, which kinetically facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. These two factors together are expected to endow Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> with fast-charging characteristics in SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202309637-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202309637-sec-0040-title">2.2 Material Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was synthesized via a solid-state reaction with elemental phosphorus acting as a structure-directing agent to modulate the morphology into an ion/electron transport-favorable 1D structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0053_R_d87477204e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Chem. Rev.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The MoS<sub>2</sub> contrast sample was obtained via a similar method (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The crystal structure of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> consisted of 1D [MoS] chains and 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> layers with each Mo atom binding with two neighboring Mo atoms to form two crystallographically independent types of Mo–Mo zigzag chains along the <i>b</i> axis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1a</a>) and the insertion of 1D [MoS] chains enlarged the interlayer spacing from 0.62 to 0.86&nbsp;nm. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and atomic force microscopy (AFM) images demonstrate that the as-prepared Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibits a nanowire morphology with an average diameter of 300&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1f</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">11</a>, Supporting Information). The Mo and S elements are distributed uniformly in the energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), and the atomic ratio of Mo and S is verified to be 2:3 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The selected area electron diffraction (SEAD) pattern in the [001] zone presents distinct single crystal diffraction signals from the (100) and (010) planes of monoclinic Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1g</a>). The annular dark-field (ADF) and annular bright-field (ABF) images of scanning transmission electron microscope (STEM) along the [001] zone reveal that the local atomic distribution and crystal structure are consistent with monoclinic Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0001">1h,i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The Riveted refined powder XRD pattern of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> shows a monoclinic structure with a P12<sub>1</sub>/m1 space group (JCPDS No. 01-089-5113) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2a</a>). The abnormal intensity enhancement of diffraction peaks of (10−1) and (110) located at 16.3° and 31.6°, respectively, demonstrate a preferred orientation along the [010] direction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0054_R_d87477204e1319" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Nano">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements are conducted to ascertain the difference in the chemical environment of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>. The Mo 3d XPS spectrum of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibits a pair of peaks located at 231.8 and 228.6&nbsp;eV, which corresponds to the Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and 3d<sub>5/2</sub> orbitals of Mo<sup>3+</sup>, respectively, with a red shift of ≈1.2&nbsp;eV compared to those of MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0055_R_d87477204e1347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Sci.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other peak located at 226.3&nbsp;eV is assigned to the S 2s orbital (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2b</a>). The S 2p XPS spectrum of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> shows a pair of peaks located at 162.8 and 161.6&nbsp;eV, which is attributed to the S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> orbitals of S<sup>2−</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Further X-ray absorption near edge spectroscopy (XANES) displays a significant red-shift of white-line, suggesting enriched electron in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2c</a>). Fourier transformed extended X-ray adsorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) reveals a slightly enlarged Mo─S bond (i.e., from ≈2.38 Å to ≈2.61 Å; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2d,e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting a delocalization state. Meanwhile, the Mo─Mo interatomic distance in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> decreases to 2.89 Å with an enlarged coordination number (CN) of ≈6.0 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), implying a faster electron transport along the Mo─Mo channel in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. In contrast, a larger Mo─Mo distance (≈3.17 Å) with a smaller CN of 5.0 can be observed in the pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> sample, indicating sluggish electron transfer kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The Raman spectrum of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> includes five characteristic peaks located at 147.8 (J<sub>1</sub>), 199.2, 223.2 (J<sub>3</sub>), 350.5 (E<sub>1g</sub>), and 436.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> (A<sub>1g</sub>), respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2f</a>). The peak at 350.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> is ascribed to the vibration of Mo atoms in octahedral coordination, which is absent in MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0056_R_d87477204e1429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nat. Commun.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202309637-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/60db0ccf-d39f-44f6-92a0-0c235ef8be80/adma202309637-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/60db0ccf-d39f-44f6-92a0-0c235ef8be80/adma202309637-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8a27c361-c4da-4bac-9624-005c6750f9dd/adma202309637-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/60db0ccf-d39f-44f6-92a0-0c235ef8be80/adma202309637-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202309637-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202309637" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Material characterizations. a) Rietveld refined XRD pattern of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. b) XPS spectra of Mo 3d and c) normalized Mo K-edge XANES spectra of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>. Mo K-edge FT-EXAFS spectrum (with phase corrections) of d) Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and e) MoS<sub>2</sub> and the corresponding fitting curves in the first coordination shell. f) Raman spectra, g) temperature-dependent resistivity curves, h) magnetic flux density-dependent Hall resistivity curves, and i) electrical conductivities and carrier concentrations at room temperature of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The electrical properties of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are further investigated by a physical property measurement system. The resistivity of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is positively correlated to temperature, indicating the metallic behavior of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2g</a>). In comparison, the resistivity curve of MoS<sub>2</sub> indicates a semiconducting behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2g</a>). The Hall resistivity measurement of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> reveals an extremely lower slope value than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>, manifesting a substantially enhanced carrier concentration (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2h</a>). Similar high carrier mobility property is observed in the VS<sub>2</sub>–VS superlattice structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0057_R_d87477204e1512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nature">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrical conductivity of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at room temperature is calculated to be 4.4 × 10<sup>4</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup>, which is 10<sup>7</sup> times larger than that of MoS<sub>2</sub> (4.5 × 10<sup>−3</sup> S m<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), and the carrier concentration of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is calculated to be 10<sup>8</sup> times larger than that of MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0002">2i</a>), verifying the validity of polaron collapse strategy. The specific surface area of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is determined to be 6.07 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> according to the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) characterization, which is expected to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> transport and electrolyte penetration (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). We further investigated the interface ion/molecule interactions related to electrolyte wetting, and the smaller contact angle of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> indicates improved surface wettability, which can assist in the formation of a high-quality electrolyte interface with low resistance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0058_R_d87477204e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - ACS Energy Lett.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202309637-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202309637-sec-0050-title">2.3 Reversible Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub></h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the detailed reaction mechanism of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, the CV measurements of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are performed for the initial three cycles within a voltage range of 0.01−3&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3a</a>). The first cathodic scan presents two peaks located at 0.90 and 0.74&nbsp;V, which correspond to the stepwise intercalation process of Na<sup>+</sup> into Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> to form Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. The pronounced peak close to 0.01&nbsp;V can be assigned to the conversion reaction that produces Na<sub>2</sub>S and metallic Mo. This process may include the formation of a cointercalation compound composed of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, Na<sup>+</sup>, and ether solvent, which has recently been observed in an ether-based electrolyte and has proven to be kinetically favorable for repeated sodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0059_R_d87477204e1631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Mater. Lett.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following anodic scan, the distinct peak located at 1.74&nbsp;V is ascribed to the reverse conversion process to form Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. With overall CV curves overlapping well after the first cycle, it can be concluded that Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> has reversible redox chemistry and low polarization.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202309637-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d9a3f501-9921-441a-8295-650b6390e3ec/adma202309637-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d9a3f501-9921-441a-8295-650b6390e3ec/adma202309637-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/99fe16d3-06c5-4c08-8c9a-0c7c7af94990/adma202309637-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d9a3f501-9921-441a-8295-650b6390e3ec/adma202309637-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202309637-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202309637" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure evolution upon sodiation and desodiation. a) CV curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the initial three cycles. b) In situ Raman spectra, c) In situ XRD patterns, and d) In situ Nyquist plots of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> in the first cycle. Ex situ Mo K-edge XANES spectra of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> during the first e) discharged and f) charged states. g) Ex situ Mo K-edge EXAFS spectra (without phase correction) of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at pristine, fully discharged, and fully charged states.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To trace the in-depth reaction mechanism of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, in situ Raman spectra, XRD, and EIS measurements are conducted at a constant current density of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> within the first cycle. In the in situ Raman spectra, Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibits two distinct characteristic Raman peaks located at 199 and 223 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with the peak at 223 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to the J<sub>3</sub> vibration mode at the initial stage (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3b</a>). The Raman peaks gradually weaken during the discharging process, corresponding to the increase in lattice disorder caused by continuous Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. The peaks disappear as the discharging process proceeds, which is ascribed to the conversion reaction. Reversibly, the characteristic Raman peaks are gradually recovered in the subsequent charging process, confirming the reconstruction of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. A similar reversible evolution of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (100) peak located at 14.5° can be observed in the in situ XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3c</a>). At stage I, the peak barely changes, suggestive of the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the vacancy sites of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Afterward, the peak shifts to a low angle at stage II, which corresponds to the lattice expansion caused by continuous Na<sup>+</sup> entry to form a new Na<sup>+</sup> insertion phase. Meanwhile, the peaks of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (10−1) and (002) located at 16.3° and 21.1°, respectively, gradually weaken without distinct shifts, which may be attributed to the large interlayer spacing n the <i>c</i> direction. At stage III, the (100) peak slightly shifts to a high angle, corresponding to an interlayer spacing reduction upon further Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. This phenomenon can be explained by the interlayer coulombic force between positive Na<sup>+</sup> and negative Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> layers, which makes the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation difficult.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0014_R_d87477204e1749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202309637-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0060_R_d87477204e1749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the charging process, a reversible peak shift of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (100) to its original position can be observed at stages IV, V, and VI, respectively, demonstrating the superior electrochemical reversibility of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. As a contrast, the in situ XRD patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub> present an irreversible (002) peak (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), manifesting partial residual Na<sup>+</sup> in the interlayers. The in situ Nyquist plots further verify the preceding process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3d</a>). The charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) decreases continuously from the open-circuit voltage (OCV) to the fully sodiated state (0.01&nbsp;V) as discharging progresses. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value in the 0.01&nbsp;V (3.8 Ω) is much lower than that in the OCV state (69.3 Ω), which can be attributed to an activation process caused by gradual electrolyte penetration and metallic Mo formation. During the charging process, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> shows an increasing trend and reaches 18.5 Ω in the fully discharged state, demonstrating the reversible redox process with superior kinetics. Notably, no obvious new semicircles corresponding to the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) are observed, which is attributed to uniform and thin SEI layers produced in the ether-based electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0061_R_d87477204e1783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Nat. Commun.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To further verify the above results, ex situ Mo K-edge XAS of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> electrodes is performed to track its valence state change and local atomic structure evolution during cycling. In the discharging process, the absorption edge of Mo K-edge XANES spectra gradually shifts to a lower energy direction along with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3e</a>), manifesting that the valence state of Mo gradually decreases. Upon being fully discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, the characteristics of the post-edge region support the formation of metallic Mo.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0015_R_d87477204e1799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After that, the absorption edge returns to the higher energy state until it almost coincides with the absorption edge of the pristine Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> in the charging state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3f</a>), indicating that the discharging and charging processes are nearly fully reversible. The Mo K-edge EXAFS spectra (without phase correction) of the pristine Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibit two obvious peaks at 1.92 and 2.67 Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0003">3g</a>), corresponding to Mo─S interaction in the first coordination shell and Mo─Mo interaction, respectively. In the fully discharged state, a strong peak appears at 2.26 Å corresponding to substantial Mo─Mo bonds in metallic Mo. In the fully charged state, the pristine Mo─S and Mo─Mo peaks recover, proving the excellent electrochemical reversibility of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The phase evolution is also confirmed by ex situ Mo 3d XPS and TEM measurements. At the OCV stage, a pair of peaks located at 231.8 and 228.6&nbsp;eV corresponds to 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Mo<sup>3+</sup> in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). At discharging to 0.01&nbsp;V, a pair of new peaks located at 230.6 (3d<sub>3/2</sub>) and 227.5&nbsp;eV (3d<sub>5/2</sub>) can be observed, corresponding to metallic Mo. The peaks of Mo 3d can be fully recovered to their pristine state in the final recharging state, demonstrating the reversible redox process. The TEM image at recharging to 3.0&nbsp;V indicates the reservation of the nanowire morphology without break (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a>, Supporting Information), and the characteristic lattice fringes corresponding to the (20−1) plane of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> can be observed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information). Even after ten cycles, most of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> remains nanowire morphology accompanied by a partial break (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the results above, it can be concluded that the fast charge transfer induced by polaron collapse results in the reversible sodium storage mechanism of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, which is expected to obtain good battery performance. The chemical equations of the sodiation process can be summarized as follows:

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202309637-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202309637-sec-0060-title">2.4 Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Performance and Kinetic Analyses</h3>
            
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is thoroughly investigated using coin-type cells. The galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> present a long plateau and irreversible capacity loss in the first cycle, after which the Columbic efficiency (CE) quickly elevates to 99% at the second cycle with a reversible charge capacity of 510 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4a</a>), consistent with the CV results. The Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode exhibits reversible capacities of 512, 480, 435, 400, 360, 300, 240, and 202 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 40, and 60 C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4b</a>), indicating much higher capacities and superior rate capabilities compared to those of MoS<sub>2</sub>. When the current density returns to 0.5 C, the capacity of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> recovers to 515 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, confirming the remarkable reversibility. The GCD curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at different current densities indicate robust rate performance and low polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4c</a>). The long-term cycling stability of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is tested at current densities of 0.5, 2, 20, and 40 C. The Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode delivers a high and stable capacity of 510 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.5 C, whereas the MoS<sub>2</sub> anode suffers a distinct capacity decline to retain a capacity of 400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4d</a>). At the current density of 2 C, Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibits a much higher reversible capacity of 430 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles than MoS<sub>2</sub> (211 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4e</a>). For ultrahigh-rate stability measurements, the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode outperforms MoS<sub>2</sub> by maintaining 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles at 20 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4f</a>). Notably, the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> exhibits a superior capacity of 217 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 C over 15&nbsp;000 cycles with nearly 100% capacity retention (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0004">4g</a>), which is among the best when compared to the reported advanced SIB anodes (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202309637-tbl-0001">1</a>). This exceptional performance can be ascribed to the fluent electron/ion migration caused by polaron collapse.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202309637-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e520754f-1bfc-4178-b056-e4904c0ad969/adma202309637-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e520754f-1bfc-4178-b056-e4904c0ad969/adma202309637-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d218ba7d-6ea2-4d92-9fe5-45a5d07291ea/adma202309637-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e520754f-1bfc-4178-b056-e4904c0ad969/adma202309637-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202309637-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202309637" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance. a) GCD curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> in the initial three cycles. b) Rate capability of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. c) GCD curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at different current densities. Cycling performance of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at current densities of d) 0.5 C, e) 2 C, f) 20 C, and g) 40 C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To systematically investigate the kinetics of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, CV measurements at various scan rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> are conducted, and the results show that all CV curves maintain a similar shape as the scan rate increases (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5a</a>). The pseudocapacitive behavior is quantitatively evaluated by using the Randles–Sevcik (R–S) equation, <i>i</i> = <i>a v<sup>b</sup></i>, in which <i>v</i> is the scan rate and <i>i</i> is the peak current.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202309637-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202309637-bib-0062_R_d87477204e2142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Adv. Mater.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A <i>b</i>-value close to 0.5 represents a diffusion-controlled process; while, a capacitance-contributed process occurs when a <i>b</i>-value is close to 1. The <i>b</i>-values of the labeled peak 1 and peak 2 are calculated to be 0.83 and 0.87, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5b</a>), suggesting a predominant capacitive characteristic of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. The pseudocapacitance and diffusion contribution can be distinguished using the equation (<i>v</i>) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. The shadow part of the CV curve indicates 91.0% of the total capacity at a scan rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5c</a>). The majority of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode can be attributed to the pseudocapacitance-controlled process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). These results indicate the superior pseudocapacitive behavior of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, which favors high-rate capability. The Nyquist plots at the OCV stage show that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (102.3 Ω) is smaller than that of MoS<sub>2</sub> (188.5 Ω), indicating a faster interfacial electron transport associated with the metallic behavior of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5e</a>). The Warburg impedance line in the low-frequency region symbolizes the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion behavior. By fitting <i>Z</i>' versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup>, the slope value of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> is calculated to be 50.1, much smaller than that of MoS<sub>2</sub> (167.4), signifying the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> relating to the low interactions with Na<sup>+</sup> in crystal material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5f</a>). In addition, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) is employed to characterize the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5g</a>). The Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode displays higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients than MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0005">5h,i</a>), further demonstrating a facilitative Na<sup>+</sup> mobility in Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202309637-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/65c22041-45f5-4802-8269-4b46b38b58c4/adma202309637-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/65c22041-45f5-4802-8269-4b46b38b58c4/adma202309637-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03a094db-4d2c-4ad5-9a50-c4297de7233d/adma202309637-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/65c22041-45f5-4802-8269-4b46b38b58c4/adma202309637-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202309637-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202309637" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical kinetics analysis. a) CV curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at various scan rates. b) Linear relationship of log (peak current) versus log (scan rate). c) Pseudocapacitive contribution of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at the scan rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Pseudocapacitive contribution at various scan rates. e) Nyquist plots and f) Linear relationship of <i>Z</i>' versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> at the OCV stage. g) GITT potential profiles and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during h) sodiation and i) desodiation process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As a 1D conductive nanowire, Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> has potential in the field of flexible electronics. We hence prepared the freestanding Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film to evaluate its application possibility in a full battery system. The sodium storage performance of the freestanding Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film was first measured by constructing the coin-type full cell by coupling a commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> and 24a, Supporting Information). The GCD curves of Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>||NVP full cell displayed a charge/discharge voltage plateau at ≈2.47/2.01&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24b</a>, Supporting Information). The full cell showed excellent rate capability for maintaining 60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a high current density of 10 C (the capacity estimate based on the total mass of the cathode and anode) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6b</a>). Upon cycling at 1 C, a reversible capacity of 80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 91% could be obtained after 150 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6b</a>). According to the time–voltage curves, the full cell could deliver a maximum energy density of 171&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 44&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high power density of 2235&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>, the energy density could remain at 95&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, the green light-emitting diode (LED) bulb was lighted up by the assembled full cell (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6b</a>). Moreover, the as-prepared Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film showed excellent flexibility, with no fractures observed after folding it several times (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6c</a>). A continuous bending and spreading measurement was used to assess the durability of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film. In particular, the conductivity of the film exhibited good stability over 100&nbsp;000-times bending–spreading cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6d</a>). We further fabricated a flexible pouch cell to evaluate its feasibility in practical applications. Due to the favorable flexibility of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film, the cell could continue to power an electronic thermometer at various deformable statuses (including flat, bending, and folding) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202309637-fig-0006">6e–h</a>). These results effectively demonstrate the huge potentiality of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanowire in flexible electronics.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202309637-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f9bcde47-1513-4f42-9fcf-5ac230f1049f/adma202309637-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f9bcde47-1513-4f42-9fcf-5ac230f1049f/adma202309637-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aa76d08a-0983-4e9c-b90f-27d36e09c99a/adma202309637-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f9bcde47-1513-4f42-9fcf-5ac230f1049f/adma202309637-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202309637-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202309637" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Flexible electrode toward full cell evaluation. a) GCD curves of the coin-type full cell based on the freestanding Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film anode and NVP cathode. b) Rate and cycling performance of the full cell. The inset is the digital image of an LED bulb powered by the full cell. c) Digital images of the freestanding Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> film during folding without fracture. d) Electrical properties measurement during the bending–spreading cycles. e–h) Digital images of the Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>||NVP flexible pouch cell driving an electronic thermometer at various deformable statuses.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202309637-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202309637-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>To summarize, ultrahigh-rate and robust sodium storage performance is achieved via chemically integrating 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> layers and 1D [MoS] chains. The insertion of 1D [MoS] chains breaks the symmetry states of 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> layers, inducing small-polaron collapse, which substantially enhances the electron/ion transport in crystal material. The results are confirmed by DFT calculations. The as-obtained Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> anode presents superior rate capability and stability for maintaining 217 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 C after 15&nbsp;000 cycles, and the application in flexible electronics is demonstrated. The proposed polaron collapse strategy induced by electron-rich insertions should make sense for high-rate electrode design.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

            <h2><a href="#" class="accordion__control" role="button" aria-controls="open-research-content" aria-expanded="false"><span class="section__title" id="open-research-section">Open Research</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a></h2>
